# 1 Introduction

Recall that a symplectic manifold is a ${ \bf 2 } n$ -dimensional smooth manifold M together with a closed nondegenerate 2-form $\omega$ . A symplectomorphism is a diffeomorphism of $M$ which preserves $\omega$ and an $\pmb { n }$ -dimensional submanifold $L \subset M$ is called Lagrangian if $\omega$ vanishes on $\mathcal { T } L$ . Such structures arise naturally from Hamiltonian dynamics and geometric optics and they have been studied for many decades.The past ten years have seen a number of important developments and major breakthroughs in symplectic geometry as well as the discovery of new links with other subjects such as dynamical systems, topology, Yang-Mils theory,theoretical physics,and singularity theory.

Many of these new developments have been motivated by Gromov's pa-per [3] on pseudoholomorphic curves in symplectic geometry. The role pseudoholomorphic curves play in Gromov's work is reminiscent of the role of self-dual Yang-Mills instantons in Donaldson's theory on smooth 4-manifolds. Gromov used pseudoholomorphic curves to prove a number of surprising and hitherto inaccessible results in symplectic geometry. For example he proved that there is no symplectic isotopy moving the unit ball in $\pmb { \mathbb { R } } ^ { 2 n }$ through a hole in a hypersurface whose radius is smaller than 1( $\pmb { a }$ symplectic camel cannot pass through the eye of a needle). The paper by McDuff and Traynor below gives a proof of this theorem which is based on Eliashberg's techniques of filling by pseudoholomorphic discs.

Moduli spaces of pseudoholomorphic curves also play an important role in McDuff's work on symplectic 4-manifolds.In her contribution below she proves a uniqueness theorem for symplectic structures on $\mathbb { C } P ^ { 2 }$ with one or two points blown up.This problem is related to the question of connectedness of the space of symplectic embeddings of two disjoint balls into $\mathbb { C } P ^ { 2 }$ .The paper by Ciriza deals with the uniqueness of symplectic structures for Kähler manifolds of nonpositive sectional curvature.

Gromov also proved that for every embedded compact Lagrangian submanifold $L \subset \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 n }$ there exists a holomorphic disc with boundary on $\pmb { L }$ (a kind of generalization of the Riemann mapping theorem). This can be interpreted as an obstruction to Lagrangian embeddings. Polterovich in his paper proves new such obstructions involving the Maslov class.

Another result by Gromov is his celebrated squeezing theorem which asserts that there is no symplectic embedding of the unit ball in $\pmb { \mathbb { R } } ^ { 2 n }$ into a cylinder $B ^ { 2 } ( r ) \times  { \mathbb { R } } ^ { 2 n - 2 }$ of radius less than 1. As a result he proved that the group of symplectomorphisms is closed with respect to the $C ^ { 0 }$ topology. Hofer interpreted Gromov's squeezing theorem as an example of symplectic invariants which he termed capacities. He discovered a number of other capacities, for example the displacement energy. In their contribution Hofer and Eliashberg prove a new inequality for the displacement energy and use this to deduce $C ^ { 1 }$ properties of a hypersurface in $\pmb { \mathsf { R } } ^ { 2 n }$ from $C ^ { 0 }$ information.

This can be viewed as an example of symplectic rigidity.

Another example of symplectic rigidity is Arnold's conjecture about the fixed points of exact symplectomorphisms (time-l-maps of Hamiltonian flows) on compact symplectic manifolds. He conjectured that the number of fixed points of such a symplectomorphism is bounded below by the sum of the Betti numbers.For the torus this was proved by Conley and Zehnder [1] using Morse theory for the symplectic action functional on the loop space. Angenent in his paper uses the symplectic action functional to give a new interpretation of Melnikov's formula for transverse intersections of stable and unstable manifolds in area preserving diffeomorphisms. Zehnder's paper deals with stability problems for symplectomorphisms of $\pmb { \mathbb { R } } ^ { 2 n }$ .For $ \textit { n } = 1$ this is related to the existence of quasi-periodic solutions which can be established by KAM theory.

An important breakthrough came when Floer combined the variational approach of Conley and Zehnder with Gromov's eliptic techniques and Witten's approach to Morse theory to prove the Arnold conjecture for monotone symplectic manifolds [2]. His work can be summarized as an infinite dimensional version of Morse theory for the symplectic action where the critical points are periodic orbits of Hamiltonian systems and connecting orbits are pseudoholomorphic curves. The resulting invariants are the Floer homology groups.A similar version of Floer homology as an invariant of homology-3- spheres is closely related to Donaldson's theory of smooth 4-manifolds.This amplifies the close relation of pseudo-holomorphic curves in symplectic manifolds with self-dual Yang-Mills equations in 4 dimensions.

The relation between symplectic geometry and gauge theory is fundamental in two of the papers.The moduli space of flat connections over a Riemann surface is a symplectic manifold on which the mapping class group acts by symplectomorphisms.The paper by Dostoglou and Salamon examines the Floer homology groups of these symplectomorphisms.An entirely different relation between contact geometry and gauge theory was discovered by Rumin and this is explained by Pansu in his contribution.

The paper by Donaldson describes new links between complex-symplectic structures on 4-dimensional cobordisms (to be thought of as a complexification of the diffeomorphism group of a 3-manifold) and Ashtekhar's formulation of the self-dual Einstein equations.

The papers by Kazarian and Lerman/Montgomery/Sjamaar deal with singularities in symplectic geometry. In the former the singularities arise from geometric optics while the latter deals with symplectic reduction in cases where the quotient is not a manifold.

The paper by Robbin and Salamon explains how the metaplectic representation can be obtained from Feynman path integrals in phase space with general quadratic Hamiltonians. This leads to a simple model of Segal's axioms for topological quantum field theory.

# References

[1] C.C.Conley and E. Zehnder,The Birkhof-Lewis fixed point theorem and a conjecture by V.I. Arnold, Inv. Math.73 (1983),33-49.

[2] A.Floer， Symplectic fixed points and holomorphic spheres,Commun. Math.Phys.120 (1989),575-611.

[3] M. Gromov, Pseudoholomorphic curves in symplectic manifolds, Invent. Math.82 (1985),307-347.

# Acknowledgements

I would like to thank the Science and Engineering Research Council and the London Mathematical Society for their generous support of the Conference at Warwick. I also would like to thank allthose who helped in running the conference,in particular Shaun Martin and Elaine Shiels.

# About this volume

This volume is based on lectures given at a workshop and conference on symplectic geometry at the University of Warwick in August 1990. The area of symplectic geometry has developed rapidly in the past ten years with major new discoveries that were motivated by and have provided new links with many other subjects such as dynamical systems, topology， gauge theory,mathematical physics and singularity theory. The conference brought together a number of leading experts in these interacting areas of mathematics.The contributions to this volume reflect the richness of the subject and include expository papers as well as original research. They will be an essential source for all research mathematicians in symplectic geometry.

# Short description

This volume contains expository and research papers by leading experts in symplectic geometry and topology. The contributions reflect the rapid developments in this area in the past ten years and the diversity of the subject. They illuminate the interactions with many other areas such as dynamical systems,topology, gauge theory, mathematical physics and singularity theory.

# A Variational Interpretation of Melnikov's Function and Exponentionally Small Separatrix Spliting

Sigurd Angenent Mathematics Department, UW Madison 2 February,1993

# $\ S 1$ . Introduction

This note is about the exponentially smallseparatrix splitting which occurs when one studies the separatrices of maps of “standard type"

$$
\Phi ( u , v ) = ( u + \varepsilon , v + \varepsilon f _ { 0 } ( u + \varepsilon v ) ) ,
$$

where $f _ { 0 }$ is an entire function, or when one considers the Poincaré-map associated with the ODE

$$
u ^ { \prime \prime } ( t ) = F ( t / \varepsilon , u ( t ) )
$$

for small values of $\varepsilon > 0$ ， and nonlinearities $\pmb { F }$ with $F ( t + 1 , u ) = F ( t , u )$ which are analytic in the $\pmb { u }$ variable. We recall that the Poincaré-map $\Phi _ { \epsilon }$ is defined in terms of the first order system

$$
u ^ { \prime } = v , v ^ { \prime } = F ( t / \varepsilon , u )
$$

which is equivalent to the second order ODE (1.1); $\Phi _ { \epsilon }$ sends $( u ( 0 ) , v ( 0 ) )$ to $( u ( \varepsilon ) , v ( \varepsilon ) )$ ，where $( u ( t ) , v ( t ) )$ ， $( 0 \leq t \leq \varepsilon )$ is a solution of (1.1). For small

$\varepsilon > 0$ the theory of averaging tells us that we may regard (1.1) as a “small" perturbation of the averaged equation

$$
u ^ { \prime \prime } = F _ { 0 } ( u )
$$

with $\begin{array} { r } { F _ { 0 } ( u ) = \int _ { \mathbf { T } } F ( \tau , u ) d \tau } \end{array}$

If the Poincaré map associated with (1.2) has a hyperbolic fixed point with a homoclinic orbit,then one expects the same to be truel for the perturbed Poincaré map $\Phi _ { \varepsilon }$ $( \varepsilon < < 1 )$ . One also expects the homoclinic orbit of perturbed map to come from a transverse intersection of the invariant manifolds through the hyperbolic fixed point. Melnikov's method allows one to verify this for smooth perturbations of (1.2) with fixed period,e.g. equations of the form $u ^ { \prime \prime } = F _ { 0 } ( u ) + \mu g ( t , u )$ .However,it has been observed that the method does not apply directly to (1.1). Holmes,Marsden and Scheurle² were the frst to try to adjust Melnikov's method to the averaging situation. They gave an asymptotic expression for the separatrix splitting if $\pmb { F }$ is of the form $F ( \tau , u ) = \sin u + \delta \varepsilon ^ { p } g ( t )$ ，with $\pmb { \mathscr { g } }$ periodic, $\pmb { p }$ suffciently large, and $\delta$ a small parameter. This result was later improved by various authors,the best result to date being due to Delshams,Teresa and Seara³.

In section 2 we give a variational interpretation of the Melnikov function,and in the subsequent sections show how this interpretation can be adapted to study the homoclinic orbits of (1.1). Like Holmes et.al. we only get an upper bound for the size of the splitting in the most general setting,while we only get transverse homoclinic intersections for a special nonlinearity, $F ( \tau , u ) = u - { ^ 3 } / { _ 2 } u ^ { 2 } + \delta \varepsilon ^ { 1 0 } H ^ { \prime } ( \tau )$ ，with $H ( \tau )$ periodic,and $\delta$ a small parameter. For this particular example the variational approach is an improvement on the results of Holmes et.al. but fails to give the result of Delshams et.al.

One advantage the variational point of view may have over others,is that it can easily be generalized, to find entire solutions of elliptic PDE's such as

$$
\Delta u = F _ { 0 } ( u ) + \mu g ( x , u ( x ) ) , \qquad u ( \infty ) = 0 ,
$$

where ${ \pmb g } ( { \pmb x } , { \pmb u } )$ is periodic in the $\pmb { x }$ -variable; given a nontrivial solution $\pmb { U } ( \pmb { x } )$ of the spatially homogeneous equation $\Delta u = F _ { 0 } ( u )$ which vanishes at ${ \pmb x } = \infty$ ， the analysis in section 2 allows one to find solutions of (1.3) close to some translate $U ( { \pmb x } + \vartheta )$ of $U ( { \pmb x } )$

Although there is no obvious Poincaré-map in this situation,one can still show4 that nondegenerate solutions of (1.3) generate many more solutions of (1.3),much in the same way that a transverse homoclinic point of the Poincaré-map generates an abundance of homoclinic orbits.

The two main examples we have in mind throughout the paper are a forced Duffing equation

$$
u ^ { \prime \prime } - F _ { 0 } ( u ) = \delta g ( t ) ,
$$

and a “kicked anharmonic oscillator”

$$
u ^ { \prime \prime } ( t ) = \varepsilon \sum _ { j \in \mathbf { Z } } \delta ( t - j \varepsilon ) \cdot F _ { 0 } ( u ( t ) )
$$

with $F _ { 0 } ( u ) = u - { ^ 3 } / { _ 2 u ^ { 2 } }$

In the second example the equation is to be interpreted in the sense of distributions: a solution is a Lipschitz function whose second distributional derivative satisfies (1.5). In fact, solutions will be piecewise linear,and their values $u _ { j } = u ( j \varepsilon )$ satisfy the recurrence relation

$$
u _ { j + 1 } - 2 u _ { j } + u _ { j - 1 } = \varepsilon ^ { 2 } F _ { 0 } ( u _ { j } ) .
$$

One easily verifies that the Poincaré-map $\Phi _ { \epsilon }$ is given by the standard type map $( u , v ) \mapsto ( u + \varepsilon v , v + \varepsilon F _ { 0 } ( u + \varepsilon v ) ) .$

In section 3 we introduce a large class of nonlinearities $\pmb { F }$ which includes both of these examples.

$\ S 2$ . A variational account of the Melnikov function We assume in this section that e =1,and that the nonlinearity Fis of the form $F ( t , u ) = F _ { 0 } ( u ) + \mu g ( t , u )$ ，where $\pmb { g }$ is some smooth function, $\mu$ is small, and ${ \cal F } _ { 0 }$ satisfies

$$
F _ { 0 } ( 0 ) = 0 , \quad F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) > 0 .
$$

This last condition implies that the origin is a hyperbolic fixed point for the local flow $\Psi _ { t }$ generated by the system $u ^ { \prime } = v , v ^ { \prime } = F _ { 0 } ( u )$

The potential energy associated with ${ \pmb F } _ { 0 }$ is given by

$$
V _ { 0 } ( u ) = - \int _ { 0 } ^ { u } F _ { 0 } ( \omega ) d \omega .
$$

We shall assume that $V _ { 0 } ( u ) < 0$ for some $u > 0$ ,and that $V _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( \alpha ) = - F _ { 0 } ( \alpha ) <$ O where $\pmb { \alpha }$ is the smallest positive root of $V _ { 0 } ( { \pmb \alpha } ) = 0$ . Under this assumption the stable and unstable manifolds $W ^ { u } , W ^ { s }$ of the origin coincide; they are parametrized by $( U ( t ) , U ^ { \prime } ( t ) )$ where $U$ is the unique positive and even solution of

$$
U ^ { \prime \prime } = F _ { 0 } ( U ) , \qquad U ( \pm \infty ) = 0 .
$$

Consider the Poincaré map $\Phi _ { \mu }$ of the perturbed system $\scriptstyle { u ^ { \prime } = v }$ ， $v ^ { \prime } { = } F ( \mu , t , u )$ If $\mu$ is small $\Phi _ { \mu }$ will have a hyperbolic fixed point ${ \mathcal { O } } _ { \mu }$ near the origin,whose stable and unstable manifolds we denote by $W _ { \mu } ^ { s } , W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ . Since $\Phi _ { \mu }$ depends smoothly on $\mu$ ,the fixed point ${ \mathcal { O } } _ { \mu }$ as well as the $W _ { \mu } ^ { s } , W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ vary smoothly with $\mu$

For most perturbations ${ \mathfrak { g } } ( t , u )$ the invariant manifolds $W _ { \mu } ^ { s } , W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ will not coincide when $\mu \neq 0$ . Melnikov's method was designed to compute the separation between the invariant manifolds for small values of $\mu$ ，and in particular,tofidthetrasverseinteectios in $W _ { \mu } ^ { s } \cap W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$

![](images/eb0166903b421db90fb434f2db3b59c7f445588a5536897ddb5fe816b6073092.jpg)

It is a commonplace’ to remark that these transverse intersections are of interest since they are known to be a cause of complicated dynamics of the Poincaré map $\Phi _ { \mu }$

We shall now proceed to describe a variational method which produces a result equivalent to Melnikov's. To begin with we construct a periodic solution which corresponds to the hyperbolic fixed point ${ \mathcal { O } } _ { \mu }$ by applying the implicit function theorem to the map $\mathcal { F } : \mathbf { R } \times C ^ { 2 } ( \mathbf { T } )  C ^ { 0 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ given by

$$
\mathcal { F } ( \mu , p ) = p ^ { \prime \prime } - F _ { 0 } ( p ) - \mu g ( t , p ) .
$$

Here $\mathbf { T } = \mathbf { R } / \mathbf { Z }$ and $C ^ { k } ( \mathbf { T } )$ is the space of $k$ times continuously differentiable functions $u ( t )$ with $u ( t + 1 ) \equiv u ( t )$

We have $\mathcal { F } ( 0 , 0 ) = 0$ while $\mathrm { d } _ { p } \mathscr { F } ( 0 , 0 )$ , the derivative of $\mathcal { F }$ w.r.t. $\pmb { p }$ ,is given by $\mathrm { D } ^ { 2 } - F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 )$ ; since $F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) > 0$ the operator $\mathrm { d } _ { p } \mathscr { F } ( 0 , 0 )$ has a bounded inverse from $C ^ { 0 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ to $C ^ { 2 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ ，and we have a smooth branch of solutions $p ( { \boldsymbol { \mu } } , \cdot ) \in C ^ { 2 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ of $\mathcal { F } ( \boldsymbol { \mu } , \boldsymbol { p } ) = 0$ with $p ( 0 , t ) \equiv 0$ . The fixed point ${ \mathcal { O } } _ { \mu }$ is now given by $\left( p ( \mu , 0 ) , p ^ { \prime } ( \mu , 0 ) \right)$ ·

Homoclinic orbits of $\Phi _ { \mu }$ correspondend to solutions $u ( t )$ of $u ^ { \prime \prime } =$ $\textstyle F ( \mu , t , u )$ which are defned for all $t \in \textbf { R }$ ，and which are asymptotic to the small solution $p ( \mu , t )$ as $t \to \pm \infty$ . To find such solutions we substitute $u ( t ) = v ( t ) + p ( \mu , t )$ and obtain the following equation for ${ \pmb v }$ ：

$$
v ^ { \prime \prime } = \hat { F } ( \mu , t , v ( t ) ) , \qquad v ( \pm \infty ) = 0 ,
$$

where

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { \hat { F } ( \mu , t , v ) = F ( \mu , t , p + v ) - p ^ { \prime \prime } } } \\ { { { } } } \\ { { = F _ { 0 } ( p + v ) - F _ { 0 } ( p ) + \mu \left\{ g ( t , p + v ) - g ( t , p ) \right\} } } \end{array}
$$

with $p = p ( \mu , t )$ ,and $\mathbf { \omega } ^ { \prime } = \partial / \partial t$ . The corresponding potential energy is given by

$$
\hat { V } ( \mu , t , v ) = \int _ { 0 } ^ { v } \hat { F } ( \mu , t , \omega ) d \omega ;
$$

it satisfies $| \hat { V } ( \mu , t , v ) | \leq C v ^ { 2 }$ for small ${ \pmb v }$ ,and hence the functional

$$
\mathcal { A } _ { \mu } ( v ) = \mathcal { A } ( \mu , v ) = \int _ { \mathbb { R } } \left( ^ { 1 } / _ { 2 } v ^ { \prime } ( t ) ^ { 2 } - \hat { V } ( \mu , t , v ( t ) ) \right) d t
$$

is well defined for $v \in H ^ { 1 } ( \mathbb { R } )$

2.1.Lemma. Critical points of $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$ are exactly the solutions of (2.3), and hence they correspond to the homoclinic orbits of $\Phi _ { \mu }$ , i.e.to the intersections f $W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ and $W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$

For small $\mu$ a critical point of $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$ is nondegenerate if and only if the Corresponding intersection of $W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ and $W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ is transverse.

Proof. The first statement holds since (2.3) is the Euler-Lagrange equation for $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$

Concerning the connection between nondegeneracy and transversality we remark first of all that the Poincaré-maps $\Phi _ { \mu }$ and $\hat { \Phi } _ { \pmb { \mu } }$ ,where the latter is derived from $w ^ { \prime \prime } = \hat { F } ( \mu , t , w )$ ,are conjugate. The conjugation is provided by the translation

$$
\tau : ( u _ { 0 } , u _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ) \mapsto ( v _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ) = ( u _ { 0 } + p ( \mu , 0 ) , u _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } + p _ { t } ( \mu , 0 ) ) .
$$

Thus if $P \in W _ { \mu } ^ { u } \cap W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ ,then $\tau ( P ) \in \hat { W } _ { \mu } ^ { u } \cap \hat { W } _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ ,and $\hat { W } _ { \pmb { \mu } } ^ { \pmb { u } }$ and $\hat { W } _ { \pmb { \mu } } ^ { s }$ intersect transversally at $\tau ( P ) \operatorname { i } \# W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ and $W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ do so at $\pmb { P }$ . We may therefore consider $\hat { W } _ { \pmb { \mu } } ^ { \pmb { u } }$ and $\hat { W } _ { \pmb { \mu } } ^ { \pmb { \mathscr { s } } }$ instead of $W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ and $W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$

Let $\textit { \textbf { v } } \in \ H ^ { 1 }$ be a critical point of $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$ .Then $v \in C ^ { \infty }$ ，and $P =$ $( v ( 0 ) , v ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) )$ is the corresponding intersection of $\hat { W } _ { \pmb { \mu } } ^ { \pmb { u } }$ and $\hat { W } _ { \pmb { \mu } } ^ { s }$ . The second derivative of $\ v { A } _ { \mu }$ at ${ \pmb v }$ is given by

$$
\mathrm { d } ^ { 2 } { \mathcal { A } } _ { \mu } ( v ) \cdot ( \varphi , \psi ) = \langle \mathrm { L } \varphi , \psi \rangle ,
$$

where ${ \bf L } : H ^ { 1 } \to H ^ { - 1 }$ is the differential operator

$$
{ \bf L } = - { \bf D } ^ { 2 } + Q ( t ) ; \qquad Q ( t ) = _ { \mathrm { d e f } } \frac { \partial \hat { F } } { \partial u } ( \mu , t , v ( t ) ) ,
$$

and where $\begin{array} { r } { \langle \varphi , \psi \rangle = \int _ { \mathbf { R } } \varphi \psi } \end{array}$

When $\mu$ is small $p ( \mu , t )$ is also small, so it follows from

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \hat { F } _ { u } ( \mu , t , v ) = F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( p ( \mu , t ) + v ) + \mu g _ { u } ( t , p ( \mu , t ) + v ) , } \end{array}
$$

$F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) > 0$ ,and $v ( \pm \infty ) = 0$ that for small $\mu$

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { t  \pm \infty } \operatorname* { i n f } _ { Q } Q ( t ) > 0 .
$$

Hence L is Fredholm with index zero for small $\mu$

Indeed, ${ \bf L } _ { 0 } = - { \bf D } ^ { 2 } + Q _ { 0 } ( t )$ with $Q _ { 0 } ( t ) = \hat { F } _ { u } ( \mu , t , 0 )$ is invertible, since inf $Q _ { 0 } ( t ) > 0 ; \mathrm { L } - \mathrm { L } _ { 0 }$ is given by multiplication with $Q ( t ) - Q _ { 0 } ( t )$ ，which vanishes at $t = \pm \infty$ and hence is a compact operator from $H ^ { 1 }$ to $H ^ { - 1 }$ ; so $\mathbf { L }$ is indeed Fredholm.

By definition the critical point ${ \pmb v }$ will be nondegenerate $\mathrm { i } f \mathbf { L } = \mathrm { d } ^ { 2 } { \mathcal { A } } _ { \mu } ( v )$ is invertible,which,due to L's Fredholmness,will be the case $\mathrm { i } \mathcal { \beta } \mathrm { L }$ is injective. The nullspace of $\mathbf { L }$ consists of those $y \in H ^ { 1 }$ which satisfy

$$
y ^ { \prime \prime } = Q ( t ) y .
$$

![](images/51d25d87c6a4b004da262227f9e6fe224e96b961e146a0c6c327c797dd4f4b74.jpg)  
Figure 2-The curve of critical points, the transverse slice,and the perturbed curve.

At $t = \pm \infty \ Q ( t )$ is bounded away from zero,so solutions of (2.6) as well as their derivatives either grow or decay exponentially. Hence there are two solutions ${ \pm } ( t )$ of (2.6) with $y _ { + } ( + \infty ) = 0$ and $y _ { - } ( - \infty ) = 0$ ,respectively.

Since (2.6) is the variational equation of (2.3),the vectors $\xi _ { + } = ( y _ { + } ( 0 ) ,$ $y _ { + } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) )$ and $\xi _ { - } = ( y _ { - } ( 0 ) , y _ { - } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) )$ span the tangent spaces $T _ { P } W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ and $T _ { P } W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ respectively.

If the intersection is not transverse,then $\xi _ { - } = \lambda \xi _ { + }$ ,and hence ${ \mathfrak { y } } _ { - } =$ $\lambda { y } _ { + }$ ； in this case $^ { y _ { + } }$ not only vanishes at $t = \infty$ ，but also at $t = - \infty$ Condition (2.5) implies that bounded solutions of (2.6) decay exponentially as $t \to \pm \infty$ ，so $^ { y _ { + } }$ belongs to the nullspace of $\mathtt { L }$ ,and ${ \pmb v }$ is a degenerate fixed point.

Conversely, if ${ \pmb v }$ is degenerate, then the ${ \pmb y } _ { \pm }$ are multiples of each other, whence the $\xi _ { \pm }$ are also multiples of each other; i.e.the intersection at $P$ is nontransverse.

Q. E. D.

For $\mu = 0$ we have a curve $\Sigma \subset H ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { R } )$ of critical points of $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$ , consisting of all translates $U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) = U ( \vartheta + t ) , \vartheta \in \mathbf { R }$ of $U$ . All these critical points are degenerate, but they are nondegenerate in the direction transverse to the curve $\Sigma$ . Indeed, define

$$
E _ { \vartheta } ^ { r } = \left\{ v \in H ^ { r } ( { \mathbf { R } } ) : \int _ { { \mathbf { R } } } v ( t ) U _ { \vartheta } { ' } ( t ) d t = 0 \right\} , \qquad r = \pm 1 .
$$

Using $U _ { \vartheta } ( \pm \infty ) = 0$ one easily verifies that $U _ { \vartheta } \in E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ . Since $U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime }$ spans the tangent space to the curve $\Sigma , E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ intersects $\Sigma$ transversally at $U _ { \vartheta }$ ·

The second derivative of $\mathcal { A } _ { 0 } = \mathcal { A } ( 0 , \cdot )$ at $U _ { \vartheta }$ is given by $\mathrm { d } ^ { 2 } \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } ) \cdot ( \varphi , \psi ) =$ $\left. \mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta } \varphi , \psi \right.$ ， where $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta } : H ^ { 1 }  H ^ { - 1 }$ is the operator $\begin{array} { r } { \mathbb { A } _ { \vartheta } = - \mathbf { D } ^ { 2 } + F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) ) } \end{array}$

2.2.Lemma. $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ is a Fredholm operator of index zero. Its kernel is spanned ${ \mathfrak { b } } y \ U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime }$ , and its range is $\overline { { E _ { \vartheta } ^ { - 1 } } }$ ·

Proof. That $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ is Fredholm follows from the same arguments we used in the previous lemma.

The kernel of $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ clearly contains $U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime }$ - just differentiate (2.2)- and by considering the growth or decay of solutions of $y ^ { \prime \prime } ( t ) = F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) ) y ( t )$ at $t = \pm \infty$ one finds that the kernel of $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ can be at most one-dimensional. Hence it must be spanned by $U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime }$

It follows that the range of $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ has codimension one,while integration by parts shows that $\mathsf { A } _ { \vartheta } \big ( H ^ { 1 } \big ) \subset E _ { \vartheta } ^ { - 1 }$ . Hence $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta } ( H ^ { 1 } ) = E _ { \vartheta } ^ { - 1 }$

Q. E. D.

# 2.3. Corollary. $U _ { \vartheta }$ is a Morse critical point of $\mathcal { A } _ { 0 } \vert \mathcal { E } _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$

It follows that the critical point $U _ { \vartheta }$ of $\mathcal { A } _ { 0 } \vert \mathcal { E } _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ will persist under small perturbations,so that we obtain a smooth family of critical points $\smash { v \vartheta , \mu } \in E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ which is defined for small $\mu$ and $\vartheta \in \mathbf { R }$

If $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$ has a critical point $v _ { * }$ near any of the $U _ { \vartheta }$ 's then $v _ { \ast } = v _ { \vartheta , \mu }$ for some $\vartheta \in \mathbf { R }$ ,but the converse is not true: not every $v _ { \vartheta , \mu }$ is a critical point of $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$ . To determine which of the $v _ { \vartheta , \mu }$ are critical points we observe that, since $v _ { \vartheta , \mu }$ is a critical point of $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$ subject to the constraint $\left. U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime } , v \right. = 0$ ,we have

$$
\mathrm { d } \mathcal { A } _ { \mu } ( v _ { \vartheta , \mu } ) = \lambda U _ { \vartheta } { } ^ { \prime } ,
$$

where $\lambda = \lambda ( \mu , \vartheta )$ is a Lagrange multiplier. This obviously implies:

2.4. Proposition. $\boldsymbol { v } _ { \vartheta , \mu }$ is a critical point of $\ v { A } _ { \mu }$ ff $\begin{array} { r } { \lambda ( \mu , \vartheta ) = 0 } \end{array}$ ·

Rather than considering $\lambda ( \mu , \vartheta )$ we introduce

$$
a ( \mu , \vartheta ) = \mathcal { A } _ { \mu } ( v _ { \vartheta , \mu } ) ,
$$

and compute

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \displaystyle \frac { \partial a } { \partial \vartheta } = \left. \mathrm { d } \mathcal { A } _ { \mu } ( v _ { \vartheta , \mu } ) , \frac { \partial v _ { \vartheta , \mu } } { \partial \vartheta } \right. } \\ { = \lambda ( \mu , \vartheta ) \left. U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime } , \frac { \partial v _ { \vartheta , \mu } } { \partial \vartheta } \right. . } \end{array}
$$

For $\mu = 0$ we have $v _ { \vartheta , \mu } = U _ { \vartheta }$ ,and hence =Us',so that wefind

$$
\lambda ( \mu , \vartheta ) = \frac { \partial a } { \partial \vartheta } ( \mu , \vartheta ) ( \Gamma + o ( 1 ) ) , \qquad ( \mu  0 ) ,
$$

with $\begin{array} { r } { \Gamma = \int _ { \mathbf { R } } U ^ { \prime } ( t ) ^ { 2 } d t > 0 } \end{array}$ . Hence for small $\mu$ the zeroes of $\lambda ( \mu , \cdot )$ and $a _ { \vartheta } ( \mu , \cdot )$ coincide.

The functional $\scriptstyle A ( 0 , v )$ is invariant under translations of ${ \pmb v }$ , i.e. under the substitution $v ( t ) \mapsto v ( t + \vartheta )$ ，so $a ( 0 , \vartheta ) = \mathcal { A } ( 0 , U _ { \vartheta } )$ does not depend on $\vartheta$ . Therefore $a _ { \vartheta } ( 0 , \vartheta ) \equiv 0$ ,and $a _ { \vartheta } ( \mu , \vartheta ) / \mu$ is a well defined smooth function for small $\mu$ ; we have

$$
a _ { \vartheta } ( \mu , \vartheta ) = \mu \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } a } { \partial \vartheta \partial \mu } ( 0 , \vartheta ) + O ( \mu ^ { 2 } ) , \qquad ( \mu  0 ) .
$$

The second derivative can be computed directly:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } a } { \partial \vartheta \partial _ { \mu } } ( 0 , \vartheta ) = \frac { \partial } { \partial \vartheta } \left( \frac { \partial } { \partial \mu } A _ { \mu } ( v _ { \mu , \vartheta } ) \right) } \\ & { \qquad = \frac { \partial } { \partial \vartheta } \left( \frac { \partial A } { \partial \mu } ( 0 , U _ { \vartheta } ) + \mathrm { d } A _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } ) \frac { \partial v _ { \mu , \vartheta } } { \partial \mu } \right) } \\ & { \qquad = \frac { \partial } { \partial \vartheta } \left( \frac { \partial A } { \partial \mu } ( 0 , U _ { \vartheta } ) \right) \qquad \mathrm { ( s i n c e ~ } \mathrm { d } A _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } ) = 0 ) } \\ & { \qquad = - \frac { \partial } { \partial \vartheta } \int _ { \mathbf { R } } \frac { \partial \hat { V } } { \partial \mu } ( 0 , t , U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) ) d t \qquad \mathrm { ( b y ~ } ( 2 . 4 ) ) } \\ & { \qquad = \int _ { \mathbf { R } } \frac { \partial \hat { F } } { \partial \mu } ( 0 , t , U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) ) U _ { \vartheta } \prime ( t ) d t . } \end{array}
$$

Using our definition of $\hat { F }$ we find that

$$
\frac { \partial \hat { F } } { \partial \mu } ( 0 , t , v ) = ( F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( v ) - F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) ) q ( t ) + g ( t , v ) - g ( t , 0 ) ,
$$

where $\begin{array} { r } { q ( t ) = \frac { \partial p } { \partial \mu } ( 0 , t ) } \end{array}$ is obtained by solving

$$
q ^ { \prime \prime } ( t ) - F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) q ( t ) = g ( t , 0 ) , \qquad q ( t + 1 ) \equiv q ( t ) .
$$

This identity implies that

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { \displaystyle \int _ { \mathbf { R } } g ( t , 0 ) U _ { \vartheta } { ' } ( t ) d t = \displaystyle \int _ { \mathbf { R } } \left( { U _ { \vartheta } } ^ { \prime \prime \prime } - F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) U _ { \vartheta } { ' } \right) q ( t ) d t } & { { } } \\ { \displaystyle } & { { } = \displaystyle \int _ { \mathbf { R } } { U _ { \vartheta } } ^ { \prime } ( t ) \left( F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) ) - F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) \right) q ( t ) d t ; } \end{array}
$$

from which we get the following formula for $a _ { \mu \vartheta }$

$$
a _ { \mu \vartheta } ( 0 , \vartheta ) = \int _ { \mathbf { R } } g ( t , U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) ) U _ { \vartheta } { } ^ { \prime } ( t ) d t .
$$

This last integral is precisely the Melnikov function. In general we can now easily prove:

2.5.Theorem (Melnikov). We assume that the Melnikov function $\mathcal { M } ( \vartheta ) = a _ { \mu \vartheta } ( 0 , \vartheta )$ has a simple zero at $\vartheta = \vartheta _ { 0 }$ . Then there exists a smooth branch of nondegenerate critical points ${ w _ { \mu } } = v _ { \mu , \vartheta ( \mu ) }$ of $\ v { A } _ { \mu }$ with ${ w _ { 0 } } = U _ { \vartheta _ { 0 } }$ · In particular,forsmall enough $\mu$ the stable andunstable manifolds $W _ { \mu } ^ { s } , W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ have a transverse intersection at $P _ { \mu } = ( w _ { \mu } ( 0 ) + p ( \mu , 0 ) , w _ { \mu } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) + p _ { t } ( \mu , 0 ) )$

Proof. We have just shown that $b ( \mu , \vartheta ) = a _ { \vartheta } ( \mu , \vartheta ) / \mu$ is a smooth function,defined for small $\mu$ ，with $b ( 0 , \vartheta ) = \mathcal { M } ( \vartheta )$ ．The implicit function theorem provides us with a smooth branch of zeroes $\vartheta ( \mu )$ of $b ( \mu , \vartheta )$ ,hence of $a _ { \vartheta } ( \mu , \vartheta )$ . Defining $w _ { \mu } = v _ { \mu , \vartheta ( \mu ) }$ as we did, we get a branch of critical points.

To show that the $w _ { \pmb { \mu } }$ are nondegenerate we consider the splitting $H ^ { 1 } =$ $E _ { \vartheta ( \mu ) } ^ { 1 } \oplus [ U _ { \vartheta ( \mu ) } ^ { \prime } ] ;$ w.r.t.this spliting wecan represent the second derivative of $\mathcal { A } _ { \mu }$ at $w _ { \pmb { \mu } }$ by

$$
\mathrm { d } ^ { 2 } A _ { \mu } ( w _ { \mu } ) = \left( \begin{array} { c c } { { \mathrm { A } _ { \vartheta ( \mu ) } | E _ { \vartheta ( \mu ) } ^ { 1 } + O ( \mu ) } } & { { O ( \mu ) } } \\ { { O ( \mu ) } } & { { a _ { \vartheta \vartheta } ( \mu , \vartheta ( \mu ) ) } } \end{array} \right) .
$$

We have seen that $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta ( \mu ) } | E _ { \vartheta ( \mu ) } ^ { 1 }$ is invertible; we have also assumed that $\vartheta _ { 0 }$ is a simple zero of $\mathcal { M }$ ,i.e. $\mathcal { M } ^ { \prime } ( \vartheta _ { 0 } ) \neq 0$ ; using $a _ { \vartheta \vartheta } ( \mu , \vartheta ( \mu ) ) = \mu \mathcal { M } ^ { \prime } ( \vartheta _ { 0 } ) + O ( \mu ^ { 2 } )$ one can then show that $\mathrm { d } ^ { 2 } { \mathcal { A } } _ { \mu } ( w _ { \mu } )$ is invertible,for small $\mu$

Q. E. D.

# - The size of the spliting -

In the classical derivation of the Melnikov function one computes the distance $d ( \mu , \vartheta )$ between $W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ and $W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ in some direction transverse to both invariant manifolds. The Melnikov function arises as the first coefficient of an expansion of $d ( { \boldsymbol { \mu } } , { \boldsymbol { \vartheta } } )$ in powers of $\mu$ $u \colon d ( \mu , \vartheta ) = \mu \boldsymbol { \mathcal { M } } ( \vartheta ) + O ( \mu ^ { 2 } )$ ，

One can show that $d ( \vartheta , \mu ) = \lambda ( \vartheta , \mu ) + { \cal O } ( \mu ^ { 2 } )$ which gives an approximate interpretation of $\scriptstyle \lambda ( \mu , \vartheta )$ . Instead we will now show that one can give an exact measure of the size of the separation between $W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ and $W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ in terms of $a ( \mu , \vartheta )$

Homoclinic points lie on the stable manifold of ${ \mathcal { O } } _ { \mu }$ so they can be ordered linearly by their position on this invariant manifold. Their position on the unstable manifold defines another ordering of homoclinic points and these two orderings need not coincide,in general.

Our construction identifies certain homoclinic points with critical points of the function $a ( \mu , \cdot )$ so that these particular homoclinics have a third ordering.

For small $\mu$ all three of these orderings coincide.That this is so becomes clear if one considers the curve $\gamma _ { \mu } ( \vartheta ) = ( v _ { \mu , \vartheta } ( 0 ) , v _ { \mu , \vartheta } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) )$ with $\vartheta \in \mathbf { R }$ . When $\mu = 0$ we have $v _ { \mu , \vartheta } ( 0 ) = U _ { \vartheta } ( 0 ) = U ( \vartheta )$ ，so that $\gamma _ { \mu } ( \vartheta ) = ( U ( \vartheta ) , U ^ { \prime } ( \vartheta ) )$ parametrizes the stable and unstable manifolds of $\mathcal { O } _ { 0 }$ ．Hence for small $\mu$ the curve parametrized by $\gamma _ { \pmb { \mu } }$ will be $C ^ { 1 }$ close to both the stable and unstable manifolds of ${ \mathcal { O } } _ { \mu }$ .Since the three orderings of critical points are determied by the order in which they occur on the curves $\gamma _ { \pmb { \mu } } , W _ { \pmb { \mu } } ^ { s }$ and ${ \cal W } _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ ， these orderings coincide for small $\mu$

2.6. Proposition. Let $P , Q \in W _ { \mu } ^ { u } \cap W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ be two consecutive homoclinic points corresponding to two consecutive critical points $\vartheta _ { P } < \vartheta _ { Q }$ of $a ( \mu , \cdot )$ Denote the region bounded by the segments of $W _ { \mu } ^ { u }$ and $W _ { \mu } ^ { s }$ that connect $\pmb { P }$ and $Q$ by $\Omega$ (some call such a region a “lobe").

Then the area of the lobe $\Omega$ is given by

$$
m ( \Omega ) = | A _ { \mu } ( w _ { P } ) - A _ { \mu } ( w _ { Q } ) | = | a ( \mu , \vartheta _ { P } ) - a ( \mu , \vartheta _ { Q } ) | .
$$

This was shown by MacKay,Meiss and Percival6.

![](images/13963d279cda082b70af46112e5f460d6ce2d12ce902ac9dc883babaa4380cad.jpg)  
Fig 3-Two adjacent homoclinic points and the corresponding lobe

The point of this observation was that it allows one to compute “the flux across a barrier,”where the barrier is formed by the stable and unstable manifolds. More precisely, choose a homoclinic point $P _ { \mu }$ corresponding to one of the critical points of $a ( \mu , \cdot )$ ；Let $W _ { \mu } ^ { s } [ P _ { \mu } , \mathcal { O } _ { \mu } ]$ and $W _ { \mu } ^ { u } [ \mathcal { O } _ { \mu } , P _ { \mu } ]$ be the segments of the two invariant manifolds between ${ \mathcal { O } } _ { \mu }$ and $P _ { \mu }$ ; and define $\Omega$ to be the bounded region these two curve segments enclose. Then the flux across $\partial \Omega$ is by definition

$$
\mathrm { f l u x } ( \Phi _ { \mu } , \Omega ) = m \left( \Omega \triangle \Phi _ { \mu } ( \Omega ) \right) .
$$

(We write $A \triangle B = ( A \cap B ^ { c } ) \cup ( A ^ { c } \cap B ) .$ ） Since one can decompose the difference $\Omega \triangle \Phi _ { \mu } ( \Omega )$ into a disjoint union of “lobes” one can actually compute the flux in terms of $a ( \mu , \cdot )$ : One finds that the fux is given by the sum of $| a ( \mu , \vartheta _ { P } ) - a ( \mu , \vartheta _ { Q } ) |$ over all consecutive critical points $\vartheta _ { P } < \vartheta _ { Q }$ in one period interval,i.e. the flux is equal to the total variation of $a ( \mu , \cdot )$ over $\mathbf { T }$ ：

$$
\mathrm { f u x } ( \Phi _ { \mu } , \Omega ) = \int _ { \mathbf { T } } \left| a _ { \vartheta } ( \mu , \vartheta ) \right| d \vartheta .
$$

For small $\mu$ the flux is therefore given by

$$
\mathrm { f l u x } ( \Phi _ { \mu } , \Omega ) = \mu \int _ { \mathbf { T } } | { \mathcal { M } } ( \vartheta ) | d \vartheta + O ( \mu ^ { 2 } ) .
$$

This completes our discussion of separatrix crossing when the perturbations are small and their period is fixed.

# $\ S 3$ .Rapidly oscillating perturbations

Weturntoeqatio(1.1),withε>OsmallInthissection wewillelai exactly what kind of equations and solutions we consider.

It is well known from the theory of averaging7 that solutions of (1.1) are approximated by those of the averaged equation,

$$
u ^ { \prime \prime } ( t ) = F _ { 0 } ( u ( t ) ) ,
$$

where

$$
F _ { 0 } ( u ) = \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } F ( \tau , u ) d \tau .
$$

Indeed, if we define

$$
G ( t , u ) = \int _ { 0 } ^ { t } \left( F ( \tau , u ) - F _ { 0 } ( u ) \right) d \tau ,
$$

then (1.1) can be rewritten as $u ^ { \prime \prime } = F _ { 0 } ( u ) + G _ { t } ( t / \varepsilon , u )$ , and hence as

$$
- u ^ { \prime \prime } ( t ) + F _ { 0 } ( u ) - \varepsilon \left( \frac { d G ( t / \varepsilon , u ( t ) ) } { d t } + G _ { u } ( t / \varepsilon , u ( t ) ) u ^ { \prime } ( t ) \right) = 0 .
$$

We shall regard (3.3) as the central equation in our discussion. This equation is a small perturbation of the averaged equation (3.1) and it is at least plausible that the deviation of solutions of (1.1) from those of the averaged equation is of order $\varepsilon$ ,on any bounded time interval; but to make a more precise statement we must first state our hypotheses.

We assume the autonomous term $ { \boldsymbol { F } } _ { 0 } ( u )$ is as in the previous section (i.e.(2.1) holds,and the corresponding potential energy is such that (2.2) again has a unique positive solution $\boldsymbol { U }$ ); in addition to this we require ${ \cal F } _ { 0 }$ to be an entire function of $u \in \mathbf { C }$

Concerning $G ( t , u )$ we assume that it is holomorphic in $\pmb { u }$ ，but only bounded measurable in the time variable. In other words,we assume that $G$ may be written as

$$
G ( t , u ) = \sum _ { j = 0 } ^ { \infty } g _ { j } ( t ) u ^ { j } ,
$$

with $g _ { j } \in L _ { \infty } ( \mathbf { T } )$ and $\begin{array} { r } { \operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } \| g _ { n } \| _ { L _ { \infty } } ^ { 1 / n } = 0 } \end{array}$ We could as say hat we require $u \mapsto G ( \cdot , u )$ to be an entire function with values in $\pmb { L } _ { \infty } ( \mathbf { T } )$

Finally,we assume that $t \mapsto G ( t , u )$ is right-continuous and vanishes at $\pmb { t = 0 }$ ; i.e.

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { t \downarrow 0 } G ( t , u ) = 0
$$

uniformly in $\pmb { u }$ ,on bounded subsets of $\mathbf { C }$

Since we do not assume that $G$ is differentiable w.r.t.the time variable, $G _ { t } ( t / \varepsilon , u ( t ) )$ cannot be defined by simply substituting $u ( t )$ in $G _ { t }$ . Hence we must define what it means for a function to be a solution of (1.1).

3.1.Definition. A solution is an absolutely continuous function $u ( t )$ which satisfes (3.3) in the sense of distributions.

The right-continuity of $G$ allows us to integrate (3.3), which leads to

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { u ( t ) = u _ { 0 } + t v _ { 0 } + \displaystyle \int _ { 0 } ^ { t } ( t - s ) \left\{ F _ { 0 } ( u ( s ) ) - \varepsilon G _ { u } ( s / \varepsilon , u ( s ) ) u ^ { \prime } ( s ) \right\} d s + } } \\ { { + \varepsilon \displaystyle \int _ { 0 } ^ { t } G ( s / \varepsilon , u ( s ) ) d s ; } } \\ { { u ^ { \prime } ( t ) = v _ { 0 } + \displaystyle \int _ { 0 } ^ { t } \left\{ F _ { 0 } ( u ( s ) ) - \varepsilon G _ { u } ( s / \varepsilon , u ( s ) ) u ^ { \prime } ( s ) \right\} d s + } } \\ { { + \varepsilon G ( s / \varepsilon , u ( s ) ) . } } \end{array}
$$

The second identity shows that the derivative $\boldsymbol { u ^ { \prime } }$ of any solution is bounded, i.e.solutions are Lipschitz continuous instead of merely absolutely continuous. The same identity also shows that $\begin{array} { r } { \operatorname* { l i m } _ { t \downarrow 0 } u ^ { \prime } ( t ) = u ^ { \prime } ( 0 + ) } \end{array}$ exists for any solution $\pmb { u }$ , so that we can speak of the initial values $( u ( 0 ) , u ^ { \prime } ( 0 + ) )$ of a solution.

By applying Picard-iteration and Gronwal's lemma to the two integral equations one can show the following.

3.2.Lemma.For any $\varepsilon \geq 0$ and $u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 } \ \in \textbf { C }$ (3.3) has a solution $u \big ( \varepsilon , u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 } ; t \big )$ on a short enough time interval $- T _ { - } ( \varepsilon , u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 } ) < t <$ $T _ { + } ( \varepsilon , u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 } )$

The $\pmb { T } _ { \pm }$ are lower semi continuous functions of $\varepsilon , u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 }$ ， the solution $u ( \varepsilon , u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 } ; t )$ depends continuously on $\varepsilon \ge 0$ , and is holomorphic in $u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 }$

Thus,if the solution $u ( t )$ of the averaged equation $u ^ { \prime \prime } = F _ { 0 } ( u )$ with $u ( 0 ) = u _ { 0 } , u ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) = v _ { 0 }$ exists on the closed interval $- t _ { - } \leq t \leq t _ { + }$ ,then for small enough $\varepsilon > 0$ (3.3) will have a solution on the same time interval, and $u ( \varepsilon ; t ) \to u ( t ) , u ^ { \prime } ( \varepsilon ; t ) \to u ^ { \prime } ( t )$ uniformly on $[ t _ { - } , t _ { + } ]$

In terms of the Poincaré-maps $\Phi _ { \epsilon }$ this may be stated as

$$
\operatorname * { l i m } _ { n  \infty } ( \Phi _ { t / n } ) ^ { n } ( u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 } ) = \Psi _ { t } ( u _ { 0 } , v _ { 0 } ) ,
$$

where $\Psi _ { t }$ denotes the flow of the averaged system $u ^ { \prime } = v , v ^ { \prime } = F _ { 0 } ( u )$

If one assumes $G ( t , u )$ does not depend on $\pmb { u }$ ,and puts $g ( t ) = G ^ { \prime } ( t )$ then one obtains the forced Duffing equation (1.2). Hence in terms of the first example we allow periodic forcing terms $g ( t )$ which are distributional derivatives of bounded measurable functions with $\begin{array} { r } { \int _ { \mathbf { T } } g ( t ) d t = 0 } \end{array}$

To obtain the second example one should choose $G ( t , u ) = Z ( t ) F _ { 0 } ( u )$ where

$$
Z ( t ) = \lfloor t \rfloor - t = \operatorname* { m a x } ( n \in \mathbf { Z } : n \leq t ) - t .
$$

In this case our previous remarks show that the solutions of the diference equation (1.4) converge to the solution of the ODE $u ^ { \prime \prime } = F _ { 0 } ( u )$ - this is ofcourse a well known fact from numerical mathematics.

# $\ S 4$ . Separatrix splitting

In computing thespliting of theseparatrices forsmallεwetrytofollow the approach of section 2 as closely as possible. The main result we shall find in this section is:

4.1. Theorem. Assume that $U$ is analytic in $\left| \mathrm { I m } t \right| < \rho _ { 0 }$ . Then for any $\rho < \rho _ { 0 }$ there exist $R ( \rho ) , \delta ( \rho ) > 0$ such that the following holds for $\varepsilon [ G ] _ { R } < \delta$

1. $\Phi _ { \epsilon }$ has a hyperbolic fixed point $\mathcal { O } _ { \epsilon }$ near the origin; $\mathcal { O } _ { \varepsilon }$ depends analytically on $\varepsilon ^ { 2 }$

2. There is a homoclinic point $P _ { \varepsilon } \in W _ { \varepsilon } ^ { s } \cap W _ { \varepsilon } ^ { u }$ such that

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathbb { H } \mathrm { u } \mathrm { x } ( \Phi _ { \epsilon } , \Omega _ { \mathcal { O } _ { \epsilon } P _ { \epsilon } } ) \leq C _ { \rho } e ^ { - 2 \pi \rho / \epsilon } , } \end{array}
$$

where $\Omega _ { \mathcal { O } _ { e } P _ { \bullet } }$ is the domain enclosed by $W _ { \epsilon } ^ { s } [ P _ { \epsilon } , \mathcal { O } _ { \epsilon } ]$ and $W _ { \epsilon } ^ { u } [ \mathcal { O } _ { \epsilon } , P _ { \epsilon } ]$ ·

In what follows we will use the following seminorms to measure the size of $\pmb { F }$ and $G$ ：

$$
[ G ] _ { R } = \mathrm { e s s . s u p } \left( | G ( t , u ) | : t \in \mathbf { T } , | u | \leq R \right) .
$$

Since $\pmb { F }$ and $G$ are analytic in the $\pmb { u }$ variable these seminorms also control the derivatives w.r.t. $u \in \mathbf { C }$ of $\pmb { F }$ and $G$ . Indeed, Cauchy's theorem implies that for almost every $\pmb { t } \in \pmb { \mathbb { T } }$

$$
\left| \frac { \partial ^ { k } G } { \partial u ^ { k } } ( t , u ) \right| \leq k ! [ G ] _ { R + 1 } ,
$$

if $| u | \leq R$

We shall frequently use this observation in the following form. If we approximate ${ { \cal F } } _ { 0 }$ or $G$ by a Taylor series,then we can estimate the error in terms of the seminorms $\left[ F _ { 0 } \right]$ and $[ G ]$ . Indeed,

$$
G ( t , u + v ) = \sum _ { k = 0 } ^ { n - 1 } \frac { \partial ^ { k } G } { \partial t ^ { k } } ( t , u ) v ^ { k } + R _ { n } ( u , v ) ,
$$

where

$$
R _ { n } ( u , v ) = v ^ { n } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 1 } { \frac { ( 1 - \lambda ) ^ { n - 1 } } { ( n - 1 ) ! } } { \frac { \partial ^ { n } G } { \partial u ^ { n } } } ( t , u + \lambda v ) d \lambda ,
$$

so that it follows from (4.1) that

$$
\begin{array} { r } { | R _ { n } ( u , v ) | \leq [ G ] _ { | u | + | v | + 1 } | v | ^ { n } . } \end{array}
$$

As in section 2 we verify that the hyperbolic fixed point at the origin persists for small $\varepsilon > 0$ ,when the equation (1.1) is perturbed.

4.2.Lemma. There is a $\delta _ { 0 } > 0$ such that (1.1) has an E periodic solution $p _ { \epsilon } ( t ) = p ( \epsilon , t / \varepsilon )$ for any $\varepsilon , G$ with $\varepsilon < \delta _ { 0 }$ and $\varepsilon [ G ] _ { 2 } < \delta _ { 0 }$ . The solution $p ( \varepsilon , t )$ is analytic in E and may be written as $\begin{array} { r } { p ( \varepsilon , t ) = \sum _ { j \geq 1 } p _ { j } ( t ) \varepsilon ^ { 2 j } } \end{array}$ . The $W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ norm of the solution $p ( \varepsilon , \cdot )$ is estimated by:

$$
\| p ( \varepsilon , \cdot ) \| \leq C \varepsilon ^ { 2 } [ G ] _ { 2 } .
$$

In terms of the Poincaré map this means that $\Phi _ { \epsilon }$ has a fixed point

$$
\mathcal { O } _ { \varepsilon } = \left( p ( \varepsilon , 0 ) , p ^ { \prime } ( \varepsilon , 0 ) / \varepsilon \right) ,
$$

for $0 < \varepsilon < \mathrm { m i n } ( \delta _ { 0 } , \delta _ { 0 } / [ G ] _ { 2 } )$ . We postpone the proof of this lemma until section 6.

After substituting $u ( t ) = v ( t ) + p ( \varepsilon , t / \varepsilon )$ in （1.1） one gets the followingequation for ${ \pmb v }$ ：

$$
v ^ { \prime \prime } = \hat { F } ( t / \varepsilon , v ( t ) )
$$

where $\hat { F } ( t , v ) = F ( t , p ( \varepsilon , t ) + v ) - \varepsilon ^ { - 2 } p ^ { \prime \prime } ( \varepsilon , t ) .$

As long as it causes no confusion we will write $p ( t )$ instead of $p ( \varepsilon , t )$ and we will also suppress the $\varepsilon$ dependence of $\hat { F }$

The averaged equation is $v ^ { \prime \prime } = \hat { F } _ { 0 } ( v )$ ， where

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \displaystyle \hat { F } _ { 0 } ( v ) = \int _ { \mathbb { T } } \left\{ F ( t , v + p ( t ) ) - \varepsilon ^ { - 2 } p ^ { \prime \prime } ( t ) \right\} d t } } \\ { { \displaystyle \qquad = \int _ { \mathbb { T } } \left\{ F _ { 0 } ( v + p ( t ) ) - G _ { u } ( t , v + p ( t ) ) p ^ { \prime } ( t ) \right\} d t . } } \end{array}
$$

The average free part of $\hat { F }$ is given by $\hat { G } _ { t } ( t , v )$ where

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \displaystyle \hat { G } ( t , v ) = \int _ { 0 } ^ { t } \Big \{ \hat { F } ( \tau , v + p ( \tau ) ) - \varepsilon ^ { - 2 } p ^ { \prime \prime } ( \tau ) \Big \} d \tau - t \hat { F } _ { 0 } ( v ) } } \\ { { \displaystyle \qquad = \int _ { 0 } ^ { t } \Big \{ F _ { 0 } ( v + p ( \tau ) ) - \hat { F } _ { 0 } ( v ) - G _ { u } ( \tau , v + p ( \tau ) ) p ^ { \prime } ( \tau ) \Big \} d \tau + } } \\ { { \displaystyle \qquad + \frac { p ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) - p ^ { \prime } ( t ) } { \varepsilon ^ { 2 } } + G ( \tau , p ( \tau ) + v ) . } } \end{array}
$$

We also define the modified potentials $\begin{array} { r } { \hat { V } _ { 0 } ( v ) = \int _ { 0 } ^ { v } \hat { F } _ { 0 } ( w ) d w } \end{array}$

Equation (4.3) is equivalent to (3.3), with ${ { F } _ { 0 } }$ and $G$ replaced by $\hat { F } _ { 0 } , \hat { G }$ Solutions of (4.3) are exactly the critical points of

$$
\mathcal { A } _ { \varepsilon } ( v ) = \int _ { \mathbb { R } } \left\{ { ^ 1 / _ { 2 } v ^ { \prime } } ( t ) ^ { 2 } - \hat { V } _ { 0 } ( v ( t ) ) - \varepsilon \hat { G } ( t / \varepsilon , v ( t ) ) v ^ { \prime } ( t ) \right\} d t .
$$

The modified nonlinearities $\hat { F }$ and $\hat { G }$ are close to $F , G$ when $\varepsilon$ is small. How close they are is measured by the following lemma.

4.3. Lemma. Let $\delta _ { 0 } > 0$ be as in lemma 4.1. Then for $\varepsilon < \delta _ { 0 } , \varepsilon [ G ] _ { 2 } < \delta _ { 0 }$ one has

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { | \hat { F } _ { 0 } ( v ) - F _ { 0 } ( v ) | \le c \left( 1 + \left[ G \right] _ { R + 2 } \right) [ G ] _ { 2 } \varepsilon ^ { 2 } . } \\ & { } & { \left| \hat { G } ( t , v ) - G ( t , v + p ( t ) ) - \frac { p ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) - p ^ { \prime } ( t ) } { \varepsilon ^ { 2 } } \right| \le c \left( 1 + \left[ G \right] _ { R + 2 } \right) [ G ] _ { 2 } \varepsilon ^ { 2 } . } \\ & { } & { | \hat { G } ( t , v ) | \le c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } } \\ & { } & { | \hat { V } _ { 0 } ( v ) - V _ { 0 } ( v ) | \le c \left( 1 + \left[ G \right] _ { R + 2 } \right) [ G ] _ { 2 } \varepsilon ^ { 2 } } \end{array}
$$

whenever $| v | \leq R$ and $| w | \leq 1$ . Here c is a constant which only depends on $\scriptstyle { R }$ and $[ F _ { 0 } ] _ { R + 2 }$

The first two inequalities are obtained from the definitions of $\hat { F } _ { 0 }$ and $\hat { G }$ and our estimate in lemma 4.1, i.e. $| p | + | p ^ { \prime } | \leq c [ G ] _ { 2 } \varepsilon ^ { 2 }$ . The third inequality then follows from the second,and one obtains the last one by integrating first estimate.

One can improve these inequalities a little by observing that we have defined $\hat { F } , \hat { G }$ and $\hat { V }$ so that

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { F _ { 0 } ( 0 ) = \hat { F } _ { 0 } ( 0 ) = \hat { G } ( t , 0 ) = 0 , } } \\ { { V _ { 0 } ( 0 ) = V _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) = \hat { V } _ { 0 } ( 0 ) = \hat { V } _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) = 0 . } } \end{array}
$$

Since all these functions are analytic in the $\pmb { u }$ variable this means that

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { \displaystyle | G ( t , v ) | \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \cdot \left| \frac v R \right| , } } \\ { { \displaystyle | \hat { V } _ { 0 } ( v ) - V _ { 0 } ( v ) | \leq c ( 1 + [ G ] _ { R + 2 } ) [ G ] _ { 2 } \varepsilon ^ { 2 } \cdot \left| \frac v R \right| ^ { 2 } } } \end{array}
$$

for $| v | \leq R$ . This has the following implications for the functional $A _ { \epsilon }$ ·

4.4. Lemma. Assume again that $\varepsilon < \delta _ { 0 } , \varepsilon [ G ] _ { 2 } < \delta _ { 0 }$ . Then $A _ { \varepsilon }$ is a holomorphic function on $H ^ { 1 } ( { \bf R } , { \bf C } )$ , which satisfies

$$
| \mathcal { A } _ { \varepsilon } ( v ) - \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( v ) | \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon ,
$$

for any $v \in H ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { R } , \mathbf { C } )$ with $\| { \pmb v } \| _ { H ^ { 1 } } \le R$ . Moreover,

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left\| \mathrm { d } ^ { k } \mathcal { A } _ { \epsilon } ( v ) - \mathrm { d } ^ { k } \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( v ) \right\| \le k ! c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon } \end{array}
$$

holds whenever $\| v \| _ { H ^ { 1 } } \leq R - 1$

Here and in the following sections we let $H ^ { r } = H ^ { r } ( { \bf R } , { \bf C } )$ stand for the Sobolev space of complex valued functions with $\pmb { r }$ derivatives in $L _ { 2 }$

Proof. For any $v \in H ^ { 1 } ( \mathbb { R } )$ one has $\| v \| _ { L _ { \infty } } \leq \| v \| _ { H ^ { 1 } }$ so that $\| \boldsymbol { v } \| _ { H _ { 1 } } \le R$ implies $\| \pmb { v } \| _ { L _ { \infty } } \le R$

The first part of this lemma now follows by applying our estimates of $\hat { G } ( t , v )$ and $\vert \hat { V } _ { 0 } ( v ) - V _ { 0 } ( v ) \vert$ to

$$
\mathcal { A } _ { \varepsilon } ( v ) - \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( v ) = \int _ { \mathbb { R } } \left\{ V _ { 0 } ( v ) - \hat { V } _ { 0 } ( v ) - \varepsilon G ( t / \varepsilon , v ) v ^ { \prime } \right\} d t .
$$

The estimate for the derivatives then follows from Cauchy's formula and the analyticity of $A _ { \epsilon } - A _ { 0 }$ ·

As in section 2 the translates of $U$ form a curve of critical points of $\scriptstyle A _ { 0 }$ .But in the present situation it turns out to be expedient to note that $U ( t )$ is an analytic function defned on a neighborhood of the real axis: Since $U ( t )$ is analytic in $S _ { \rho _ { 0 } } = \{ t \in \mathbf { C } : | \mathrm { I m } t | < \rho _ { 0 } \}$ ,every translate $U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) = U ( \vartheta + t )$ with $\vartheta \in S _ { \rho _ { 0 } }$ will also be a critical point of $\scriptstyle A _ { 0 }$ . The curve $\Sigma$ of critical points is therefore a holomorphic curve in the complex Hilbert space $H ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { R } , { \bf C } )$ ·

We define $E _ { \vartheta } ^ { \pm 1 }$ to be the subspaces of $H ^ { \pm 1 }$ consisting of all $\pmb { w }$ with $\begin{array} { r } { \int _ { \mathbb { R } } w U _ { \vartheta } { ' } = 0 } \end{array}$ . The reader should note that for non-real values of $\vartheta$ this space is the $L _ { 2 }$ -orthogonal complement of the complex conjugate of $U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime }$ rather than $U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime }$ itself.With this definition the spaces $E _ { \vartheta } ^ { \pm 1 }$ depend holomorphically on $\vartheta$

Analogously to lemma 2.2 one now shows

4.5. Lemma. $\mathrm { A } _ { \vartheta } = \mathrm { d } ^ { 2 } \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } ) : H ^ { 1 }  H ^ { - 1 }$ is Fredholm with index zero.

$\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta } | E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ is injective, the range of $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ is $\boldsymbol { E } _ { \vartheta } ^ { - 1 }$ ， its kernel is spanned by $U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime }$ ·

$U _ { \vartheta }$ is a nondegenerate critical point of $\mathcal { A } | \mathcal { E } _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$

The stability of nondegenerate critical points implies that any small perturbation of $\mathcal { A } _ { 0 } \vert \mathcal { E } _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ will have a nondegenerate critical point near $U _ { \vartheta }$ In particular, $\pmb { \mathcal { A } } _ { \pmb { \epsilon } } | \pmb { \mathcal { E } } _ { \pmb { \vartheta } } ^ { 1 }$ will have such a critical point $\boldsymbol { v } _ { \vartheta , \epsilon } \in E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ if $\varepsilon$ is small enough.

4.6. Lemma. For any $\rho < \rho _ { 0 }$ there is $\iota \ \delta = \delta ( \rho ) > 0$ such that $\mathcal { A } _ { \epsilon } \vert E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ has a unique critical point $v _ { \epsilon , \vartheta } \in E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ with

$$
\| v _ { \epsilon , \vartheta } - U _ { \vartheta } \| _ { H ^ { 1 } } \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon
$$

for any $\vartheta \in S _ { \rho }$ . Here $R = 1 + \operatorname* { s u p } _ { \vartheta \in S _ { \rho } } \left\| U _ { \vartheta } \right\| _ { H ^ { 1 } }$ ， and c is a constant that only depends on $\pmb { \rho }$ and $[ F _ { 0 } ] _ { R + 2 }$ ·

The $v _ { \epsilon , \vartheta }$ are holomorphic in $\vartheta$ , and E periodic in the sense that $v _ { \epsilon , \vartheta + \epsilon } ( t )$ $= v _ { \vartheta , \epsilon } ( t + \varepsilon )$ ·

As $H ^ { 1 }$ valued function of $\vartheta$ they are also $C ^ { 1 }$ close to $U _ { \vartheta }$ ， with

$$
\left\| \frac { \partial v _ { \epsilon , \vartheta } } { \partial \vartheta } - { U _ { \vartheta } } ^ { \prime } \right\| _ { H ^ { 1 } } \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon .
$$

We shall obtain $\scriptstyle v _ { \vartheta , \varepsilon }$ from the Lagrange multiplier equation

$$
\mathrm { d } \mathcal { A } _ { \epsilon } ( v ) = \lambda { U _ { \vartheta } } ^ { \prime } , \qquad \langle v , { U _ { \vartheta } } ^ { \prime } \rangle = 0
$$

in section 7.

Once again the $v _ { \pmb { \mathscr { O } } , \pmb { \vartheta } }$ are not necessarily critical points of $A _ { \epsilon }$ ，but any critical point of $A _ { \epsilon }$ which is close to some $U _ { \vartheta }$ must be a $v _ { \pmb { \tau } , \pmb { \vartheta } }$ . At the same time,a $v _ { \epsilon , \vartheta }$ is a critical point $\mathrm { i } \pmb { \mathscr { f } }$ the corresponding Lagrange multiplier $\lambda ( \varepsilon , \vartheta )$ ,defined by (4.4),vanishes.

Consider $a ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ) = \mathcal { A } _ { \varepsilon } ( U _ { \vartheta } + w _ { \epsilon , \vartheta } )$ . Then $a ( \varepsilon , \vartheta + \varepsilon ) = a ( \varepsilon , \vartheta )$ for all $\vartheta \in S _ { \rho }$

Just as in section 2 the critical points of $a ( \varepsilon , \cdot )$ correspond precisely to the homoclinics of the Poincaré-map. Indeed, for $\vartheta \in S _ { \rho }$ and $\varepsilon [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \leq \delta ( \rho )$ we have

$$
\frac { \partial a } { \partial \vartheta } ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ) = \lambda ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ) \left. U _ { \vartheta } ^ { \prime } , \frac { \partial v _ { \varepsilon , \vartheta } } { \partial \vartheta } \right. = \lambda ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ) \left( \Gamma + O ( \varepsilon [ G ] _ { R + 2 } ) \right) ,
$$

where $\begin{array} { r } { \Gamma = \int _ { \mathbf { R } } U ^ { \prime } ( t + \vartheta ) ^ { 2 } d t > 0 } \end{array}$ does not depend on $\vartheta$

Unlike the case of the Melnikov function we cannot expand $a ( \varepsilon , \vartheta )$ in powers of $\varepsilon$ . For the moment we shall be satisfied with an upper estimate for the total variation of $a ( \varepsilon , \cdot )$

We begin with a crude estimate:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { | a ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ) - \mathcal { A } ( U _ { \vartheta } ) | \leq | \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } + { w } _ { \varepsilon , \vartheta } ) - \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } ) | + | \mathcal { B } _ { \varepsilon } ( U _ { \vartheta } + { w } _ { \varepsilon , \vartheta } ) | } \\ & { \qquad \leq \underset { \| \tau \| \leq R + 2 } { \operatorname* { s u p } } | \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( z ) | \cdot \| { w } _ { \varepsilon , \vartheta } \| ^ { 2 } + c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon } \\ & { \qquad \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon . } \end{array}
$$

Expand $a ( \varepsilon , \vartheta )$ in a Fourier series: if

$$
a ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ) = \sum _ { n } a _ { n } \exp ( 2 \pi i n \vartheta / \varepsilon ) ,
$$

then

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \displaystyle \left| a _ { n } \right| \le e ^ { - 2 \pi | n | \rho / \epsilon } \frac { 1 } { \varepsilon } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \varepsilon } \left| a ( t \pm i \rho ) \right| d t } } \\ { { \le c \varepsilon [ G ] _ { R + 2 } e ^ { - 2 \pi | n | \rho / \varepsilon } . } } \end{array}
$$

Hence for real valued $\vartheta$ we get:

$$
| a _ { \vartheta } ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ) | \leq \frac { 2 \pi } { \varepsilon } \sum _ { n } n | a _ { n } | \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } e ^ { - 2 \pi \rho / \varepsilon } .
$$

Define the region $\Omega _ { \mathcal { O } _ { \epsilon } P _ { \epsilon } }$ as at the end of section 2: i.e. let $P _ { \varepsilon }$ be a homoclinic point associated with a zero of $a _ { \vartheta } ( \varepsilon , \cdot )$ . Such a point certainly exists,since ${ \mathfrak { a } } ( \varepsilon , \vartheta )$ is a periodic function of $\vartheta -$ simply take a point $\vartheta \in \mathbf { R }$ where $a ( \varepsilon , \cdot )$ is maximal, or minimal. Now let $\Omega _ { \mathcal { O } _ { \epsilon } P _ { \epsilon } }$ be the bounded region enclosed by $W _ { \epsilon } ^ { s } [ P _ { \epsilon } , \mathcal { O } _ { \epsilon } ]$ and $W _ { \epsilon } ^ { u } [ \mathcal { O } _ { \epsilon } , P _ { \epsilon } ]$ . Then we find that

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } {  { \mathrm { f l u x } ( \Phi _ { \varepsilon } , \Omega ) \leq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \varepsilon } \vert a _ { \vartheta } ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ) \vert d \vartheta } } \\ & { } & { \leq c \vert G \vert _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon e ^ { - 2 \pi \rho / \varepsilon } . } \end{array}
$$

# \$5. Holomorphic Contraction Mapping Lemma

In what follows we shallfrequently use the contraction mapping lemma to obtain solutions of differential equations. Since we are working with holomorphic maps of Banach spaces the usual contraction mapping lemma admits a stronger formulation.In practice this stronger version will not allow us to prove anything we couldn't do with the usual version,but it does save us a small amount of work: We no longer have to go through the calculations which show that the maps we consider are contractions.

5.1.HCM-lemma. Let $\pmb { \cal E }$ be a complex Banach space, and let $f : B _ { r } \to$ $B _ { \theta r }$ be a holomorphic mapping, where $B _ { \rho } = \{ x \in E : \| x \| < \rho \}$

f $\theta < { ^ 1 / _ { 2 } }$ ，then $f | B _ { \theta r }$ is a contraction, and hence has a unique fixed point in $B _ { \theta r }$

1f $\pmb { F }$ is another complex Banach space, and $f : F \times E \to E$ is holomorphic such that $f ( \vartheta _ { 0 } , \cdot )$ maps $B _ { r }$ into $B _ { \theta r }$ for some $\theta < \%$ ， $\vartheta _ { 0 } \in F$ ，then $f ( \vartheta , \cdot )$ has a unique fixed point ${ \pmb x } ( \vartheta ) \in B _ { \theta r }$ for $\vartheta \in F$ close enough to $\vartheta _ { 0 }$ This fixed point depends holomorphically on $\vartheta$ ，and

$$
\lVert \mathrm { d } x ( \vartheta _ { 0 } ) \rVert \leq \frac { 1 - \theta } { 1 - 2 \theta } \lVert \mathrm { d } _ { \vartheta } f ( \vartheta _ { 0 } , x ( \vartheta _ { 0 } ) ) \rVert .
$$

Proof. Use Cauchy's inequality to estimate the derivative of $f$ on $B _ { \theta r }$ in terms of its sup norm on $B _ { r }$ ：

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \| \mathrm { d } f ( x ) \| \le \displaystyle \frac { 1 } { \rho } \operatorname* { s u p } \left( \| f ( y ) \| : \| y - x \| < \rho \right) } \\ & { \qquad \le \displaystyle \frac { \operatorname* { s u p } \left( \| f ( y ) \| : y \in B _ { r } \right) } { r - \| x \| } } \\ & { \qquad \le \displaystyle \frac { \theta r } { r - \theta r } = \frac { \theta } { 1 - \theta } } \\ & { \qquad < 1 , } \end{array}
$$

where we chose $\rho = r - \| x \|$ ,and used $\theta < { } ^ { 1 } / _ { 2 }$ . Thus $f$ is a contraction on $B _ { \theta r }$ which must have a fixed point.

By the implicit function theorem we see that the fxed point $\pmb { x } ( \vartheta )$ depends smoothly (i.e.holomorphically） on parameters; the estimate for $\mathbf { d } x ( \vartheta _ { 0 } )$ follows directly after differentiating $x ( \vartheta ) = f ( \vartheta , x ( \vartheta ) )$

Q. E. D.

5.2.HCM-lemma, special case. If the holomorphic map $f : E \to E$ satisfes

$$
\| f ( x ) \| \leq a + b \| x \| ^ { 2 } ,
$$

with $a b < 1 / 1 6$ ，then $f$ has a unique fixed point $\pmb { x _ { 0 } }$ with $\| { \pmb x } \| \leq 2 a$

The special case follows from the HCM-lemma,after observing that $f ( B _ { 4 a } ) \subset B _ { 4 \theta a }$ ， where $\theta = ( 1 + 1 6 a b ) / 4 < ^ { 1 } / _ { 2 }$

# \$6.Proof of lemma 4.1

We define W1(T) to be the spaceof allcomplex valued Lipschitz continuous functions on $\mathbf { T }$ . By Rademacher's theorem $W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ consists of those distributions on $\mathbf { T }$ whose first derivative can be represented by a bounded measurable function. $W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ is a complex Banach space,with norm

$$
\| f \| _ { W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } } = _ { \operatorname* { d e f } } \| f \| _ { L _ { \infty } } + \| f ^ { \prime } \| _ { L _ { \infty } } .
$$

We define $W _ { \infty } ^ { - 1 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ to be the space of all distributions $f$ which may be written as $f = g + h ^ { \prime }$ , for certain $g , h \in L _ { \infty } ( \mathbf { T } )$ . With the following norm:

$$
\| f \| _ { W _ { \infty } ^ { - 1 } } = _ { \operatorname* { d e f } } \operatorname* { i n f } \left( \| g \| _ { L _ { \infty } } + \| h \| _ { L _ { \infty } } : f = g + h ^ { \prime } \right)
$$

$W _ { \infty } ^ { - 1 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ is a complex Banach space. One can identify this space as the dual of $W _ { 1 } ^ { 1 } ( { \bf T } )$ ,the space of absolutely continuous functions on $\mathbf { T }$

One substitutes $u ( t ) = p ( t / \varepsilon )$ in (1.1),or (3.3)and finds the following equation for $\pmb { p }$ ：

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathrm { D } ^ { 2 } p ( s ) - \varepsilon ^ { 2 } F ( s , p ( s ) ) = 0 , } \end{array}
$$

where the linear operator $\mathbf { D ^ { 2 } } : W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { T } ) \to W _ { \infty } ^ { - 1 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ is given by $\mathrm { D } ^ { 2 } p ( s ) =$ $p ^ { \prime \prime } ( s )$ . This operator is bounded, Fredholm; its kernel consists of allconstant functions, its range is $X ^ { - } = \left. q \in W _ { \infty } ^ { - 1 } ( \mathbf { T } ) : \int _ { \mathbf { T } } q = 0 \right.$ . When restricted to $X ^ { + } = X ^ { - } \cap W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { T } ) \ \mathbf { D } ^ { 2 }$ is injective. We denote its inverse by $\mathbb { K } : X ^ { - } \to X ^ { + }$ ， and extend it to an operator $\mathrm { K } : W _ { \infty } ^ { - 1 } ( \mathbf { T } ) \to X ^ { + } \subset W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ by defining ${ \mathrm { K } } c = 0$ for any constant function $\pmb { c }$

Write $p ( s )$ as $p ( s ) = c + q ( s )$ ，where $\pmb { c }$ is constant and $\begin{array} { r } { \int _ { \mathbf { T } } q ( s ) d s = 0 } \end{array}$ Then ${ \pmb q } ^ { \prime \prime } = { \pmb p } ^ { \prime \prime }$ , so that application of $\mathbf { K }$ to (6.1) leads to

$$
q = - \varepsilon ^ { 2 } \mathrm { K } \left\{ F _ { 0 } ( c + q ( s ) ) + G _ { t } ( s , c + q ( s ) ) \right\} = _ { \mathrm { d e f } } \varphi _ { \varepsilon } ( c , q ) .
$$

Here $\varphi _ { \epsilon }$ is a holomorphic map from $\mathbf { C } \times X ^ { + }$ to $X ^ { + }$ . If $| c | \leq 1 , \| q \| \leq 1$ then $\varphi _ { \epsilon }$ can be estimated by

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left\| \varphi _ { \varepsilon } ( c , q ) \right\| \le C \varepsilon ^ { 2 } \left( \left| c \right| + \left\| q \right\| + \left[ G \right] _ { 2 } \right) , } \end{array}
$$

where $\| \cdot \|$ denotes the $W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } ( \mathbf { T } )$ norm.Applying the HCM-lemma,it follows that for small enough $\varepsilon$ and any $| \mathbf { c } | \leq 1$ there is a unique small solution $\scriptstyle q _ { \epsilon , c }$ of $q = \varphi _ { \epsilon } ( c , q )$ . This solution is a holomorphic $X ^ { + }$ -valued function of $\pmb { c }$ and $\varepsilon$ ,and satisfies

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \| q \| \leq C \varepsilon ^ { 2 } \left( | c | + [ G ] _ { 2 } \right) . } \end{array}
$$

The function $c + q _ { \epsilon , c }$ will satisfy (6.1） upto a constant. Requiring this constant to vanish will give us one additional equation,which determines $\pmb { c }$ To get this equation we integrate (6.1),and write $F _ { 0 } ( u ) = F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) u + F _ { 2 } ( u ) u ^ { 2 }$ This leads to

$$
c = \frac { - 1 } { F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) } \int _ { \mathbb { T } } \left\{ F _ { 2 } ( p ) p ^ { 2 } - G _ { u } ( s , p ) q ^ { \prime } \right\} d s = _ { \mathrm { d e f } } \ \psi _ { \varepsilon } ( c ) .
$$

Here $p = \pmb { c } + q _ { \epsilon , \pmb { c } }$ , and we have replaced $G _ { t } ( s , p )$ by $( G ( s , p ) ) ^ { \prime } - G _ { u } ( s , p ) p ^ { \prime }$ ， Using (6.3) we get the following estimate for $\psi _ { \pmb { \varepsilon } }$ ：

$$
| \psi _ { \varepsilon } ( c ) | \leq C | c | ^ { 2 } + C \varepsilon ^ { 2 } [ G ] _ { 2 } ^ { 2 } ,
$$

f $\varepsilon [ G ] _ { 2 }$ is small then we can again apply the HCM-lemma and conclude that $\psi _ { \epsilon }$ has a fixed point $\pmb { c } _ { \pmb { \varepsilon } }$ with

$$
\begin{array} { r } { | c _ { \epsilon } | \le C \varepsilon ^ { 2 } [ G ] _ { 2 } ^ { 2 } . } \end{array}
$$

We obtain the desired solution by adding $\pmb { c }$ and $q \colon p ( \varepsilon , \cdot ) = c _ { \varepsilon } + q _ { \varepsilon , c _ { \varepsilon } }$ . That $p ( \varepsilon , \cdot )$ only contains even powers of $\varepsilon$ follows from the uniqueness of the solution for fixed $\varepsilon$ ,and from the fact that the original equation (6.1) does not change if one changes the sign of $\varepsilon$

# $\ S 7$ .Proof of Lemma 4.5

Consider theEuler-Lgrangeequation(4.4). In this equation wesubtitte $\pmb { v } = \pmb { U } _ { \pmb { \vartheta } } + \pmb { w }$ ，we write $\mathcal { A } _ { \varepsilon } ( v ) = \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( v ) + \mathcal { B } _ { \varepsilon } ( v )$ ,and we expand $\mathbf { d } \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( v )$ ina Taylor series around $U _ { \vartheta }$ ：

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathrm { d } \mathcal { A } _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } + w ) = w ^ { \prime \prime } + F _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } + w ) - F _ { 0 } ( U _ { \vartheta } ) } \\ & { \qquad = \left\{ - \mathrm { D } ^ { 2 } + F _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ( U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) ) \right\} w + R _ { 2 } ( U _ { \vartheta } , w ) } \\ & { \qquad = \mathrm { A } _ { \vartheta } w + R _ { 2 } ( U _ { \vartheta } , w ) } \end{array}
$$

where $R _ { 2 }$ may be estimated by (4.2),i.e. if $| U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) | \le R$ and $| w | \leq 1$ ，then $| R _ { 2 } ( U _ { \vartheta } ( t ) , w ) | \leq [ F _ { 0 } ] _ { R + 2 } | w | ^ { 2 }$

Thus $\boldsymbol { w } = \boldsymbol { v } - \boldsymbol { U } _ { \vartheta }$ satisfies

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathsf { A } _ { \pmb { \vartheta } } \pmb { w } + R _ { 2 } ( U _ { \pmb { \vartheta } } , \pmb { w } ) + \mathrm { d } \mathcal { B } _ { \pmb { \varepsilon } } ( U _ { \pmb { \vartheta } } + \pmb { w } ) = \lambda U _ { \pmb { \vartheta } } ^ { \prime } , \qquad \langle \pmb { w } , U _ { \pmb { \vartheta } } ^ { \prime } \rangle = \mathsf { 0 } . } \end{array}
$$

Let $\mathbf { T } _ { \vartheta } : H ^ { - 1 }  H ^ { 1 }$ be the pseudo inverse of $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ ; i.e.

$$
\mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta } \big | E _ { \vartheta } ^ { - 1 } = \left( \mathrm { A } _ { \vartheta } \big | E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 } \right) ^ { - 1 } , \quad \mathrm { a n d } \quad \mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta } { U _ { \vartheta } } ^ { \prime } = 0 .
$$

The range of $\mathbf { T } \vartheta$ is $E _ { \vartheta } ^ { 1 }$ . One can compute $\mathbf { T } _ { \vartheta }$ from the resolvent of $\mathbf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ via the following formula&

$$
\mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta } = \frac { - 1 } { 2 \pi i } \oint _ { | \zeta | = r } \left( \zeta - \mathrm { A } _ { \vartheta } \right) ^ { - 1 } \frac { d \zeta } { \zeta } .
$$

Here $r > 0$ should be chosen small enough,so that $\zeta - \mathsf { A } _ { \vartheta }$ has a bounded inverse for any $\zeta$ with $0 < | \zeta | \leq r$

This expression for $\mathbf { T } _ { \vartheta }$ shows that $\mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta }$ depends holomorphically on $\vartheta \in$ $S _ { \rho _ { 0 } }$ and that for any $0 < \rho < \rho _ { 0 }$

$$
\operatorname* { s u p } _ { \vartheta \in S _ { \rho } } \| \mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta } \| _ { H ^ { - 1 }  H ^ { 1 } } = C _ { \rho } < \infty .
$$

Now we apply $\mathbf { T } _ { \vartheta }$ to both sides of (7.1) to get

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \boldsymbol { w } = - \mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta } \left\{ R _ { 2 } ( \boldsymbol { U } _ { \vartheta } , \boldsymbol { w } ) + \mathrm { d } \mathcal { B } _ { \varepsilon } ( \boldsymbol { U } _ { \vartheta } + \boldsymbol { w } ) \right\} } \\ & { \quad = _ { \mathrm { d e f } } \varphi ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ; \boldsymbol { w } ) . } \end{array}
$$

Clearly $\varphi ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ; \cdot )$ is a holomorphic map of $H ^ { 1 }$ to itself. If $\| U _ { \vartheta } \| \leq R - 1$ and $\| w \| \leq 1$ then we have

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \| \varphi ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ; w ) \| \leq \| \mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta } \| \left\{ \| R _ { 2 } ( U _ { \vartheta } , w ) \| _ { H ^ { - 1 } } + \| \mathrm { d } \mathcal { B } _ { \varepsilon } ( U _ { \vartheta } + w ) \| _ { H ^ { - 1 } } \right\} } \\ & { \qquad \leq C _ { \rho } \left\{ [ F _ { 0 } ] _ { R + 2 } \| w \| ^ { 2 } + c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon \right\} } \\ & { \qquad \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon + c \| w \| ^ { 2 } , } \end{array}
$$

where $\pmb { c }$ only depends on $\rho , R$ and $[ F _ { 0 } ] _ { R + 2 }$ . By the HCM-lemma there is a $\delta _ { 1 } > 0$ such that $\varphi ( \varepsilon , \vartheta ; \cdot )$ has a unique fixed point $w _ { \epsilon , \vartheta }$ with

$$
\| w _ { \epsilon , \vartheta } \| \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon
$$

as long as $[ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon < \delta _ { 1 }$

It is clear from the definition of $\varphi ( \varepsilon , \vartheta , w )$ that $\varphi$ is holomorphic in $\vartheta$ ， with

$$
\frac { \partial \varphi } { \partial \vartheta } = - \frac { \partial \mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta } } { \partial \vartheta } \left\{ \cdots \right\} - \mathrm { T } _ { \vartheta } \left\{ \frac { \partial R _ { 2 } ( \boldsymbol { U } _ { \vartheta } , \boldsymbol { w } ) } { \partial \boldsymbol { U } _ { \vartheta } } \boldsymbol { U } _ { \vartheta } \prime + \mathrm { d } ^ { 2 } \boldsymbol { \mathcal { B } } _ { \epsilon } ( \boldsymbol { U } _ { \vartheta } + \boldsymbol { w } ) \boldsymbol { U } _ { \vartheta } \prime \right\} .
$$

Thus

$$
\left\| \frac { \partial \varphi } { \partial \vartheta } \right\| \leq c \left( [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon + \| w \| ^ { 2 } \right) ,
$$

and the HCM-lemma implies that $w _ { \pmb { \epsilon } , \pmb { \vartheta } }$ is also holomorphic in $\vartheta$ ，with $\begin{array} { r } { \| \partial _ { \vartheta } w _ { \epsilon , \vartheta } \| \leq c [ G ] _ { R + 2 } \varepsilon } \end{array}$

# $\ S 8$ . The forced Duffing equation

Sofar we haven't found an explicit formulafora(e,0),forsmalle. Inthis section we show how one can do this for the forced Duffing equation

$$
u ^ { \prime \prime } - u + ^ { 3 } / { } _ { 2 } u ^ { 2 } = g ( t / \varepsilon ) ,
$$

provided $g ( t ) = G ^ { \prime } ( t )$ ,and $\| G \| _ { L _ { \infty } }$ is small enough:

8.1. Theorem. If $g ( t ) = \delta \varepsilon ^ { 1 0 } H ^ { \prime } ( t )$ where $\| \cal H \| \in \cal L _ { \infty } ( \mathbf { T } )$ satisfes $\begin{array} { r } { \int _ { \mathbf { T } } H = } \end{array}$   
0, then

$$
\frac { \partial a } { \partial \vartheta } = \delta \varepsilon ^ { 8 } e ^ { - 2 \pi ^ { 2 } / \varepsilon } \{ - 3 2 \pi ^ { 3 } | \hat { H } _ { 1 } | \sin ( \frac { 2 \pi \vartheta } { \varepsilon } - \phi ) + o ( 1 ) \} , \qquad ( \delta , \varepsilon  0 )
$$

uniformly in $\vartheta \in \mathbf { R }$ ， where $\hat { H } _ { 1 }$ is the first Fourier coefficient of $H ( t )$ ,and$\phi = \arg \hat { H } _ { 1 }$ ·

f $\hat { H } _ { 1 } \neq 0$ then, if ε and $\delta$ are small enough, $\mathbf { \delta } _ { \pmb { a } , \pmb { \vartheta } }$ has precisely two nondegenerate zeroes in any period interval, and hence the Poincare-map $\Phi _ { \delta , \epsilon }$ has two transverse homoclinic orbits.

# - The small solution -

Let $p ( \varepsilon , \tau )$ be the small solution, i.e. $p ( \varepsilon , \tau + 1 ) \equiv p ( \varepsilon , \tau )$ and $p ( t ) = p ( \varepsilon , t / \varepsilon )$ satisfies (8.1). Since the perturbation $G ( t , u ) \equiv G ( t )$ does not depend on $\pmb { u }$ , all the seminorms $[ G ] _ { R } = \| G \| _ { L _ { \infty } }$ are the same, and $\pmb { p }$ satisfies $\| p \| _ { L _ { \infty } } \leq$ $c \| G \| _ { L _ { \infty } } \varepsilon ^ { 2 }$ ，provided $\| G \| _ { L _ { \infty } } \varepsilon < \delta _ { 0 }$ . To calculate $\pmb { a } _ { \pmb { \vartheta } }$ for small $\varepsilon$ ， it seems that we need to now the first order term in the expansion of $\pmb { p }$

8.2. Lemma. $\| p + \varepsilon ^ { 2 } \mathrm { K } g \| _ { W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } } \le C \varepsilon ^ { 4 } \| g \| _ { W _ { \infty } ^ { - 1 } }$ ， where the operator $\mathbf { K }$ isas in section 5.

Since $\textstyle \int _ { \mathbf { T } } H = 0$ , it follows from the way $\mathbf { K }$ was defined that $\left( \mathbb { K } H ^ { \prime } \right) ^ { \prime } = H$ ， so this lemma implies that

$$
\| p ^ { \prime } + \delta \varepsilon ^ { 1 2 } H \| _ { L _ { \infty } } \leq C \delta \varepsilon ^ { 1 4 } .
$$

Proof. As in section 6 we write $p = \pmb { c } + \pmb { q }$ with $\textstyle \int _ { \mathbf { T } } q = 0$ and $\pmb { c }$ a constant. In view of (6.2),

$$
\begin{array} { r } { q = - \varepsilon ^ { 2 } \mathrm { K } \left\{ g + F _ { 0 } ( p ) \right\} , } \end{array}
$$

and the estimate for $\| \boldsymbol { p } \| _ { W _ { \infty } ^ { 1 } }$ ,this implies

$$
